  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_75d6aef3-9f0c-4c7c-a357-9985d1e04b4f'
      });

<span id="freeTextContainer8750894378571419424">This work of art reflects life in Ireland at the turn of the last century, and by rejecting euphemism, reveals to the Irish their unromantic realities. Each of the 15 stories offers glimpses into the lives of ordinary Dubliners, and collectively they paint a portrait of a nation.</span>

<a href="/questions/578087-how-does-joyce-answer-the-age-old">How does Joyce answer the age-old question, &quot;What time is it?&quot;</a>
</div>
<div class='communityQAFooter questionFooter'>
<ul class='questionFooterActions'>
<li class='footerItem like'>
<span class='likeItContainer' id='like_container_community_question_578087'><a rel="nofollow" class="jsLike like_it" href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/new?remember=true&amp;return_url=%2Fbook%2Fshow%2F11012.Dubliners">like</a></span>

<a href="/questions/578087-how-does-joyce-answer-the-age-old">5 years ago</a>
</li>
<li class='footerItem communityQuestionPermalink'>
<a href="/questions/578087-how-does-joyce-answer-the-age-old">Add your answer</a>
</li>

</div>
<div class='allCommunityAnswers'>
<div class='communityAnswer'>
<div class='communityAnswerBody'>
<div class='answererUserIcon'>
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/14710388-wordsmitten"><img alt="" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/users/1353280647i/14710388._UY100_CR18,0,100,100_.jpg" /></a>
</div>
<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/14710388-wordsmitten">WordSmitten</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<div class='spoiler'>
This answer contains spoilers…
<span class='spoilerAnswerText'>
<a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[
  
    For Joyce, time closely connects the local population to the quantity of liquid flowing at the village pub. "...Farrington suggested the Scotch House.<a href="#" rel="nofollow noopener">…more</a>
    For Joyce, time closely connects the local population to the quantity of liquid flowing at the village pub. "...Farrington suggested the Scotch House. The bar was full of men and loud with the noise of tongues and glasses." <br />The equation is "time + night + booze = escape from the mundane" and my father, who was 100-proof Irish, understood and lived by the equation. Joyce and his canon of literary works are part of the DNA, even today. <a href="#" rel="nofollow noopener">(less)</a>
  
 <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>
</span>
</div>
</span>
</div>

<div id="review_1814299459" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1814299459" />
    <a title="Sean Barrs " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/27788046-sean-barrs"><img alt="Sean Barrs " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1605281953p2/27788046.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1814299459?book_show_action=true">Nov 17, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sean Barrs " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sean Barrs " href="/user/show/27788046-sean-barrs">Sean Barrs </a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/23291.Dubliners">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27788046-sean-barrs?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27788046-sean-barrs?shelf=4-star-reads">4-star-reads</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27788046-sean-barrs?shelf=short-stories">short-stories</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27788046-sean-barrs?shelf=modernist-movement">modernist-movement</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1814299459" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13746573192087932344">Life is full of missed opportunities and hard decisions. Sometimes it’s difficult to know what to actually do. <i> Dubliners </i> creates an image of an ever movie city, of an ever moving exchange of people who experience the reality of life. And that’s the whole point: realism. Not everything goes well, not everything is perfectly constructed. Life is random and unpredictable. If we’re not careful it may escape from us entirely. <br/><br/>There are two types of stories in <i> Dubliners. </i>The first, and by far the m</span>
  <span id="freeText13746573192087932344" style="display:none">Life is full of missed opportunities and hard decisions. Sometimes it’s difficult to know what to actually do. <i> Dubliners </i> creates an image of an ever movie city, of an ever moving exchange of people who experience the reality of life. And that’s the whole point: realism. Not everything goes well, not everything is perfectly constructed. Life is random and unpredictable. If we’re not careful it may escape from us entirely. <br><br>There are two types of stories in <i> Dubliners. </i>The first, and by far the most effective, are those associated with despair, nihilism and death. The second type deals with more ordinary aspects of modern life, the representation of the city and social exchanges. As a collection they provide an image of dark, murky city struggling to cope with the problems associated with rapid urbanisation. The stories do not intertwine, but you are left with the impression that they are not that far from each other: their proximity feels close as you read further into each one. <br><br>The true mastery of Joyce’s writing reveals itself in what he doesn’t say, the subtle suggestions, the lingering questions, as each story closes without any sense of full resolution. And, again, is this not true of real life? In narrative tradition there is a structured beginning, middle and end, but in the reality of existence it doesn’t quite work this way. Life carries on. It doesn’t have a form of narrative closure, a convenient wrapping up of plot, after each wound we take in life. It carries on. We carry on. And for the Dubliners isolation carries on. <br><br><b>
  <i>“He could not feel her near him in the darkness nor hear her voice touch his ear. He waited for some minutes listening. He could hear nothing: the night was perfectly silent. He listened again: perfectly silent. He felt that he was alone.” </i>
</b><br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1479385187i/21183425._SX540_.jpg" width="400" height="200" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></span>

<div id="review_309776245" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/309776245" />
    <a title="Ahmad Sharabiani" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/614778-ahmad-sharabiani"><img alt="Ahmad Sharabiani" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1194930432p2/614778.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/309776245?book_show_action=true">Apr 10, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ahmad Sharabiani" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ahmad Sharabiani" href="/user/show/614778-ahmad-sharabiani">Ahmad Sharabiani</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780140042221" href="/book/show/321594.Dubliners">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/614778-ahmad-sharabiani?shelf=irish">irish</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/614778-ahmad-sharabiani?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/614778-ahmad-sharabiani?shelf=short-stories">short-stories</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/614778-ahmad-sharabiani?shelf=literature">literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/614778-ahmad-sharabiani?shelf=20th-century">20th-century</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer309776245" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8063833884307367174"><b>Dubliners, James Joyce </b><br/><br/>In his stories, Joyce combines heterogeneous elements. Poetic mysticism is expressed in a naturalistic way. They pay attention to sound and melody for illustration. In their works, they always use humor and irony and references to myths and holy books. If the reader can grasp all these mysteries, he will be glad that he may not be able to read any other work. <br/><br/>Joyce is a language engineer before he became a writer. Joyce's particular view of language, and the word, as the c</span>
  <span id="freeText8063833884307367174" style="display:none"><b>Dubliners, James Joyce </b><br /><br />In his stories, Joyce combines heterogeneous elements. Poetic mysticism is expressed in a naturalistic way. They pay attention to sound and melody for illustration. In their works, they always use humor and irony and references to myths and holy books. If the reader can grasp all these mysteries, he will be glad that he may not be able to read any other work. <br /><br />Joyce is a language engineer before he became a writer. Joyce's particular view of language, and the word, as the cells that make up the body of the story, is so profound and original that critics are still struggling to uncover the vague layers of his stories. <br /><br />The sections are hidden side by side in new words, invented by Joyce himself. There are two completely different opinions about Joyce. Some consider him a complex lunatic. That his conflict with language has led him astray, and others who say he has unparalleled talent, which is beyond human comprehension today. <br /><br />Joyce's innovation in language is unbelievable. Not only do they bring to life the ancient words of their language; They also make words in their works. Sometimes, words with more than a hundred letters, or a combination of several words, that make up a word, show a multiple sense. Multi-layered words that tell and convey several secrets. According to Joyce, the world is in bad shape. In which lowly joys and poverty and depravity threaten human life. The book embraces and embraces a collection of fifteen short stories, including issues such as Irish history; Human beings; Death; Love; Life; Fear and ...; Have written.<br /><br />Dubliners is a collection of fifteen short stories by James Joyce, first published in 1914. They form a naturalistic depiction of Irish middle class life in and around Dublin in the early years of the 20th century.<br /><br />The stories:<br /><br /><b>The Sisters</b> – After the priest Father Flynn dies, a young boy who was close to him and his family deals with his death superficially.<br /><br /><b>An Encounter</b> – Two schoolboys playing truant encounter a middle-aged man.<br /><br /><b>Araby</b> – A boy falls in love with the sister of his friend, but fails in his quest to buy her a worthy gift from the Araby bazaar.<br /><br /><b>Eveline</b> – A young woman weighs her decision to flee Ireland with a sailor.<br /><br /><b>After the Race</b> – College student Jimmy Doyle tries to fit in with his wealthy friends.<br /><br /><b>Two Gallants</b> – Two con men, Lenehan and Corley, find a maid who is willing to steal from her employer.<br /><br /><b>The Boarding House</b> – Mrs Mooney successfully manoeuvres her daughter Polly into an upwardly mobile marriage with her lodger Mr Doran.<br /><br /><b>A Little Cloud</b> – Little Chandler's dinner with his old friend Ignatius Gallaher casts fresh light on his own failed literary dreams. The story also reflects on Chandler's mood upon realising that his baby son has replaced him as the centre of his wife's affections.<br /><br /><b>Counterparts</b> – Farrington, a lumbering alcoholic scrivener, takes out his frustration in pubs and on his son Tom.<br /><br /><b>Clay</b> – The old maid Maria, a laundress, celebrates Halloween with her former foster child Joe Donnelly and his family.<br /><br /><b>A Painful Case</b> – Mr Duffy rebuffs Mrs Sinico, then, four years later, realises that he has condemned her to loneliness and death.<br /><br /><b>Ivy Day in the Committee Room</b> – Minor politicians fail to live up to the memory of Charles Stewart Parnell.<br /><br /><b>A Mother</b> – Mrs Kearney tries to win a place of pride for her daughter, Kathleen, in the Irish cultural movement, by starring her in a series of concerts, but ultimately fails.<br /><br /><b>Grace</b> – After Mr Kernan injures himself falling down the stairs in a bar, his friends try to reform him through Catholicism.<br /><br /><b>The Dead</b> – Gabriel Conroy attends a party, and later, as he speaks with his wife, has an epiphany about the nature of life and death. At 15–16,000 words this story has also been classified as a novella. The Dead was adapted into a film by John Huston, written for the screen by his son Tony and starring his daughter Anjelica as Mrs. Conroy.<br /><br />عنوانها: «دوبلینیها»؛ «مردگان»؛ «دوبلینی ها و نقد دوبلینی ها»؛ نویسنده: جیمز جویس؛ تاریخ نخستین خوانش: روز پانزدهم اکتبر سال 1984میلادی<br /><br />عنوان: دوبلینی ها؛ نویسنده: جیمز جویس؛ مترجم: پرویز داریوش؛ تهران، اشرفی، 1346؛ در 227 ص؛ چاپ دیگر: انتشارات آبان؛ 1362؛ چاپ دیگر: تهران، اساطیر، 1371؛ در 214 ص؛ شابک: 9643312410؛ موضوع: داستانهای کوتاه از نویسندگان ایرلندی - سده 20م<br /><br />مترجم: محمدعلی صفریان، تهران، نیلوفر، چاپ نخست 1372، در 300ص و 143ص؛ چاپ دوم 1378؛ چاپ سوم، 1383؛ چاپ پنجم 1388؛ شابک 9789644481024؛ دوبلینی ها ص 1، تا ص 300، ترجمه صفریان، و ص 1، تا ص 143، آینه ای در راه، مقالاتی در نقد دوبلینیها با ترجمه صالح حسینی<br /><br />مترجم: صالح حسینی، تهران، نیلوفر، چاپ نخست 1389، در 453ص؛ شابک 9789644484681؛ <br /><br />مترجم: سولماز واحدی کیا؛ تهران، کوله پشتی؛ 1389؛ در 200ص؛ شابک 9786005337976؛ <br /><br />با عنوان: مردگان؛ مترجم: علیرضا متین نیا؛ مشهد، سخن گستر؛ 1389؛ در 228ص؛ شابک 9789644778551؛ <br /><br />مترجم: امیر علیجانپور؛ تهران، آوای مکتوب؛ 1394؛ در 232ص؛ شابک 9786007364208؛ <br /><br />گویا همین پانزده داستان کوتاه را با عنوان: «بهترین داستانهای کوتاه جیمز جویس»؛ با ترجمه جناب احمد گلشیری انتشارات نگاه در سال 1388؛ در 402ص منتشر کرده است<br /><br />جویس در داستانهایش، عناصر ناهمگون را باهم درمی‌آمیزند؛ عرفان شاعرانه را، با شیوه ی ناتورالیستی، بیان میکنند، برای تصویرپردازی به صدا و آهنگ صدا، توجه دارند؛ در آثارشان، هماره، از طنز و کنایه و اشاره به اساطیر، و کتاب‌های مقدس، سود می‌برند؛ خوانشگر اگر بتواند این همه رمز و کنایه را دریابد، به لذتی می‌رسد، که شاید از خوانش هیچ اثر دیگری نتواند؛ «جویس» پیش از آن که نویسنده باشند، یک مهندس زبان هستند؛ نگاه ویژه‌ ی «جویس» به زبان، و واژه، به عنوان سلول‌های تشکیل‌ دهنده‌ ی بدنه‌ ی داستان، چنان ژرف و بدیع است، که هنوز منتقدان، درگیر کشف لایه‌ های مبهم داستان‌های ایشان هستند؛ بخش‌هایی که در لا‌ به‌ لای کلماتی نو، که خود «جویس» اختراع کرده، پنهانند؛ در باره «جویس»، دو نظر کاملاً مخالف وجود دارد؛ عده‌ ای او را، دیوانه‌ ی مغلق‌ گو می‌دانند، که درگیری‌ اش با زبان، او را به بیراهه کشانده، و دیگرانی که میگویند؛ ایشان استعدادی بی‌نظیر دارند، که از درک انسان امروز فراتر است؛ نوآوری «جویس» در زبان، ناباورانه است؛ ایشان نه تنها واژه‌ های کهن زبان خویش را زنده می‌کنند؛ بلکه در آثارشان واژه‌ سازی نیز می‌کنند؛ گاه، واژگانی با بیش از صد حرف، و یا ترکیبی از چندین کلمه، که یک کلمه را تشکیل می‌دهد، تا حسی چندگانه را نشان دهد؛ واژه‌ گانی چند لایه که چندین راز را بازمیگویند و می‌رسانند؛ به باور «جویس» دنیا بد مخمصه‌ ای ‌است، که در آن شادی‌های حقیر و فقر و رذالت، زندگی انسانها را تهدید می‌کند؛ کتاب مجموعه ای از پانزده داستان کوتاه را، در بر و در آغوش خویش گرفته که در آنها به مسایلی نظیر: تاریخ ایرلند؛ انسانها؛ مرگ؛ عشق؛ زندگی؛ ترس و... می‌پردازند<br /><br />بیشتر شخصیت‌های داستان‌های این مجموعه، دوباره در کتاب اولیس فرا خوانده می‌شوند؛ داستان از نثر بسیار قدرتمندی برخوردار است، و جزو شاهکارهای ادبی به شمار است؛ مجموعه داستان یک سیر ادبی را از ابتدا تا انتها در بر می‌گیرد که به داستان بلند مردگان ختم می‌شود؛ <br /><br />اسامی داستان‌های کوتاه: <br />خواهرها: کشیش فلین می‌میرد و پسر جوان که همراه با خانواده‌ اش برای مراسم ختم او آمده‌ اند یاد خاطرات و کارهای کشیش می‌افتد...؛ <br /><br />برخورد: یک بچه از مدرسه بیرون می‌رود...؛ <br /><br />عربی: پسری عاشق دختری در محله‌ شان می‌شود، او به بازار عربی می‌رود تا برای دختر هدیه‌ ای بخرد...؛ <br /><br />اولین: دختری خانواده‌ اش را ترک می‌کند تا همراه با ملوانی برود...؛ <br /><br />همتایان؛ <br /><br />پس از مسابقه: مردی با دوست و همدرسه‌ ای قدیمی خود روبرو می‌شود...؛ <br /><br />دو زن‌ نواز: دو مرد زنی را دنبال می‌کنند تا با او طرح دوستی بریزند...؛ <br /><br />ابری کوچک: مردی همراه با دوست قدیمی‌ اش مشغول خوردن ناهار است و به یاد آرزوهایی که داشته میافتد<br /><br />یک روز در ستاد انتخابات: کارکنان یک ستاد انتخاباتی دور هم گرد آمده‌ اند و از پارنل یکی از رهبران مبارزات ایرلند یاد می‌کنند...؛ <br /><br />گل؛ <br /><br />پانسیون؛ <br /><br />یک حادثهٔ دردناک؛ <br /><br />مردگان؛ <br /><br />تاریخ بهنگام رسانی 06/07/1399هجری خورشیدی؛ ا. شربیانی</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer7369988050419877064">Was James Joyce the greatest English language writer in modern times? <br/><br/>I don’t know, maybe, but Dubliners helps to make his case. <br/><br/>Brilliant in it’s subtle, realistic way. <br/><br/>Fifteen stories that paint a portrait of Dublin at the turn of last century. "The Dead" is the final story and the most poignant and powerful but several stand out as exceptional, and they are all good. <br/><br/>“Counterparts” is a disturbing close up look at the old drunken Irish family stereotype that fails to be humorous. “A Mother” t</span>
  <span id="freeText7369988050419877064" style="display:none">Was James Joyce the greatest English language writer in modern times? <br><br>I don’t know, maybe, but Dubliners helps to make his case. <br><br>Brilliant in it’s subtle, realistic way. <br><br>Fifteen stories that paint a portrait of Dublin at the turn of last century. "The Dead" is the final story and the most poignant and powerful but several stand out as exceptional, and they are all good. <br><br>“Counterparts” is a disturbing close up look at the old drunken Irish family stereotype that fails to be humorous. “A Mother” though epitomizes the stereotype of a blusterous, stubborn as a mule Irish mother. And about those Irish stereotype? Might they have been given voice by Joyce through Dubliners? <br><br>A highly influential work from a respected, inspiring author - this is great reading.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1450839508i/17475845._SX540_.jpg" width="400" height="213" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></span>

<div id="review_1062300998" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1062300998" />
    <a title="Garima" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7915003-garima"><img alt="Garima" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1360053697p2/7915003.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1062300998?book_show_action=true">Sep 23, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Garima" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Garima" href="/user/show/7915003-garima">Garima</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/23291.Dubliners">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7915003-garima?shelf=sing-a-song">sing-a-song</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7915003-garima?shelf=my-2-cents">my-2-cents</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7915003-garima?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7915003-garima?shelf=to-re-read">to-re-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7915003-garima?shelf=wehmut">wehmut</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7915003-garima?shelf=short-stories">short-stories</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1062300998" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8876935726613644496"><br/>Before embarking towards my maiden Joyce read, I prepared myself to pour in as much effort required on my part to understand Dubliners. I didn’t assume them to be incomprehensible or distant, but an anxiety akin to meeting a known stranger for the first time was definitely present. The said anxiety shortly materialized into a much-awaited prospect after reading the opening story and finally transformed into a confident and gentle companion who led me through the sepia streets of an unassuming ci</span>
  <span id="freeText8876935726613644496" style="display:none"><br />Before embarking towards my maiden Joyce read, I prepared myself to pour in as much effort required on my part to understand Dubliners. I didn’t assume them to be incomprehensible or distant, but an anxiety akin to meeting a known stranger for the first time was definitely present. The said anxiety shortly materialized into a much-awaited prospect after reading the opening story and finally transformed into a confident and gentle companion who led me through the sepia streets of an unassuming city. Dublin, as I soon realized, was just around the corner. <br /><br /><i> I had hardly any patience with the serious work of life which, now that it stood between me and my desire, seemed to me child’s play, ugly monotonous child’s play.</i><br /><br />Calmly engaged within the secure air of its daily affairs, the people of Dublin were also ostensibly calm and secure and yet a moment reflection about a dormant or potential life managed to extract stories which were snuggled in simple form and simpler titles but traced intricate and at times, unheeded emotions. An aimless walk concluded in cheap happiness and an embarrassing accident convinced someone to search for an elusive redemption. A death unveiled the value of oblivious living while a motherly conduct was driven by frustrations and misplaced ambitions. Most of these characters were representative, not whole but of a remarkable fragment of lives that we either experience ourselves or witness in others during the time we live. <br /><br /><i> She sat amid the chilly circle of her accomplishments, waiting for some suitor to brave it and offer her a brilliant life.</i><br /><br />A perpetual struggle for attention between past and present was an integral part of these stories sans any violent clashes. Some of them appeared as if being viewed from a neighbor’s window and some welcomed me through a cordial door and took their time to introduce every element of the household. I admired how well the majority of people were coping with the consequences of their choices and how easily they found humor in the ironies of life. And I quailed on seeing the suffocation of the negligible minority on being caught in the web of their inhibitions. I understood that even after getting a crystal clear view of their circumstances from a vantage point, they still refused to adopt a different course, to sail away to a different country, to a dreamy world.<br /><br /><i>It was hard work – a hard life – but now that she was about to leave it she did not find it a wholly undesirable life.</i><br /><br />With every subsequent narration, I imagined Joyce to be in deep contemplation about everything and everyone around him. I imagined him to carefully select an appropriate frame for his various thoughts and placing each one of them at their desirous place. I imagined how he must have wanted to capture an epiphanic moment among the melancholic tune of Irish songs, when he wanted to paint a picture with decided title but undecided colors; or when he simply wished to write about the approachable beauty of that girl on other side of the pavement. I imagined his joy for the love and pain at the criticism for his native place. I was left in awe of the virtuosity of this young man and the several portraits he created with his words. <br /><br /><i>He had an odd autobiographical habit which led him to compose in his mind from time to time a short sentence about himself containing a subject in the third person and a predicate in the past tense.</i><br /><br />And when I reached the end, I simply wished to possess a literary talent like this for a very short time to write a story of my own and discreetly slip it into this collection. Dublin and Dubliners felt that close to me.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="8876935726613644496" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer980110763" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13152159710575346680"><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1404905701i/10295337._SY540_.jpg' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/>(*)<br/><br/><br/>This is a collection of short stories. Or are they one single long story? <b> “A Portrait of the City as an Old and Stultifying Enclave.”</b>?<br/><br/>This story fashions a kaleidoscopic vision of Dublin in the early 1900s. This is a city enclosed in a gray cylinder that a hand turns periodically and new scenes are conjured up for the contemplation of a single (male) eye. The same components reappear, falling in different places playing different relationships with each other; some others disappear forever o</span>
  <span id="freeText13152159710575346680" style="display:none"><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1404905701i/10295337._SY540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>(*)<br><br><br>This is a collection of short stories. Or are they one single long story? <b> “A Portrait of the City as an Old and Stultifying Enclave.”</b>?<br><br>This story fashions a kaleidoscopic vision of Dublin in the early 1900s. This is a city enclosed in a gray cylinder that a hand turns periodically and new scenes are conjured up for the contemplation of a single (male) eye. The same components reappear, falling in different places playing different relationships with each other; some others disappear forever or stay hidden in the corners to may be reappear again after all. One cannot know how the elements will place themselves on the next turn.<br><br>Rich collection of elements: youth and adulthood – money matters – trapping marriages – trapping love – ill-conceived duties – Mary – temptations for youth – the ghost of England – the public house – chattered dreams – Jesuits – alcohol – nationalism – unfeminine women – dreams of change – school ploys – Death – Parnell – liberating escape – topographical anchorage of the streets of Dublin.<br><br>Another turn. And there is Dublin again.<br><br>And each time we recognize the narrow spaces, the sombre, the dreary, the faded, the routine, and the bleak prospects.<br><br>The drabness of many of these hovering elements is however transformed by a play of incantation. The desolation is perplexingly denatured into elegance and the stark absence of sentimentality blooms because what it renders is so very genuine. There is a magic wand in the form of a pen of wizardry that by the clothing with words, precisely chosen words, carefully written words, encapsulates the dreariness and creates tales that captivate and enchant us.<br><br>And may be there is also an additional light in this kaleidoscope that makes these sorry elements shine through those inner reflecting mirrors. <br><br>The humour of a sparkling and luminous mind.<br><br><br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1404906157i/10295404._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>(**)<br><br><br>----------------<br><br>(*) Citiscape. Rachel Simonson, US.<br><br>(**) Anthropocene. David Thomas Smith, Ireland.<br></span>

<div id="review_1739340473" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1739340473" />
    <a title="Duane" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/24389390-duane"><img alt="Duane" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1403316391p2/24389390.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1739340473?book_show_action=true">Aug 26, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Duane" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Duane" href="/user/show/24389390-duane">Duane</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/24389390-duane?shelf=2016-book-challenge">2016-book-challenge</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/24389390-duane?shelf=reviewed-books">reviewed-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/24389390-duane?shelf=50-greatest-short-stories">50-greatest-short-stories</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/24389390-duane?shelf=rated-books">rated-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/24389390-duane?shelf=irish">irish</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/24389390-duane?shelf=short-stories">short-stories</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1739340473" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12425166342718140723">James Joyce once said; "If Dublin suddenly disappeared from the Earth it could be reconstructed out of my book Ulysses". I have never been to Dublin so I have no idea what it's like today, but through Joyce's writings I have a sense of what it was like in the early 20th century. It’s not so much that he describes the physical city, but his descriptions of its establishments, its social and political atmosphere, and especially its people, is so detailed and complete that the physical picture just</span>
  <span id="freeText12425166342718140723" style="display:none">James Joyce once said; "If Dublin suddenly disappeared from the Earth it could be reconstructed out of my book Ulysses". I have never been to Dublin so I have no idea what it's like today, but through Joyce's writings I have a sense of what it was like in the early 20th century. It’s not so much that he describes the physical city, but his descriptions of its establishments, its social and political atmosphere, and especially its people, is so detailed and complete that the physical picture just "pops up", like in one of those children's pop up books. It is so in Ulysses and it certainly is true in this book, Dubliners.<br /><br />Dubliners, this collection of 15 short stories, was published in 1914, two years before <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7588.A_Portrait_of_the_Artist_as_a_Young_Man" title="A Portrait of the Artist as a Young Man by James Joyce" rel="nofollow noopener">A Portrait of the Artist as a Young Man</a> and eight years before <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/338798.Ulysses" title="Ulysses by James Joyce" rel="nofollow noopener">Ulysses</a>. These stories lay the groundwork for his later novels, a primer, if you will. I think it's good advice to anyone just starting on James Joyce works, to start with <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/11012.Dubliners" title="Dubliners by James Joyce" rel="nofollow noopener">Dubliners</a>. Like all short story collections some are better than others, but they are all good, all consistent, and they never stray from Joyce's verbal painting of his beloved Dublin.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12425166342718140723" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3583700916" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3583700916" />
    <a title="JimZ" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/99765491-jimz"><img alt="JimZ" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1575257175p2/99765491.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3583700916?book_show_action=true">Oct 06, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="JimZ" class="user" itemprop="url" name="JimZ" href="/user/show/99765491-jimz">JimZ</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780199536436" href="/book/show/3831780-dubliners">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3583700916" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9078722836928948966">I was put off by reading James Joyce because I was scared of reading him — that I wouldn’t understand a damn thing he said although I knew he was a brilliant writer…one for the ages. I think it was ‘Ulysses’ that scared me off, and I made a massive generalization (if I don’t understand that book, I won’t understand anything by Joyce). My mistake. <br/><br/>I remember a Goodreads friend recommended I read it, because I think I or they had read a short story collection (whose author escapes me right now), a</span>
  <span id="freeText9078722836928948966" style="display:none">I was put off by reading James Joyce because I was scared of reading him — that I wouldn’t understand a damn thing he said although I knew he was a brilliant writer…one for the ages. I think it was ‘Ulysses’ that scared me off, and I made a massive generalization (if I don’t understand that book, I won’t understand anything by Joyce). My mistake. <br /><br />I remember a Goodreads friend recommended I read it, because I think I or they had read a short story collection (whose author escapes me right now), and they said there was some similarity of ‘Dubliners’ to the short story collection we were discussing. So, I procured a copy and was blown away. My copy was an issue by Oxford World’s Classics. There were oodles of footnotes to each story near the back of the book, and after I read a short story I would then go the back of the book and read the footnotes (not every footnote but a large number of them). I learned a lot via the footnotes, and found them to be very interesting. <br /><br />There were 15 stories, and as I read, I took notes and rated each story — I’ll just list the ratings next to the stories (average is 3.8 stars but add in the Introduction, an alternative translation of ‘Sisters’, and the footnotes and it adds up to 5 by my reckoning. 😊 ). <br />•	Sisters: 4 stars<br />•	An Encounter: 3.5 stars<br />•	Araby: 4 stars<br />•	Eveline: 4.5 stars<br />•	After the Race: 2 stars<br />•	Two Gallants: 3 stars<br />•	The Boarding House: 5 stars<br />•	A Little Cloud: 4 stars<br />•	Counterparts: 4.5 stars<br />•	Clay: 3.5 stars<br />•	A Painful Case: 5 stars<br />•	Ivy Day in the Committee Room: 2 stars<br />•	A Mother: 3.5 stars<br />•	Grace: 4 stars<br />•	The Dead: 5 stars<br /><br />That last story ‘The Dead’ has to be one of the best short stories I have read in a long time. So much to pack in it (it was about 40 pages long). The last page in which the husband Gabriel is thinking about the young man who once loved his wife, and she him, before Gabriel came onto the scene was just … so sad, so beautifully written. What a wonderful way to end the short story collection… “His soul swooned slowly as he heard the snow falling faintly through the universe and faintly falling, like the descent of their last end, upon all the living and the dead.”<br /><br />I learned where “beyond the pale” came from. Up until today, I was clueless. <br />•	‘The pale’ was the name given in the 14th century to that part of Ireland over which England exercised jurisdiction before the whole was conquered; centered on Dublin, it varied in extent at different times from the reign of Henry II until full conquest under Elizabeth I’ in ‘in the pale’, ‘pale’ connotes ‘civilization’ or ‘civilized behavior’; here, it means specifically ‘conceding in his behavior the authority of the Church’ and ironically inverts the historical meaning where the ‘wild’ Irish Catholic native population existed ‘beyond the pale’; they now, of course, figuratively represent ‘the pale’ itself (referred to in ‘Grace’).<br /><br />I didn’t know in Catholicism that The Immaculate Conception (mother of Jesus having conceived although a virgin), though a generally held belief from the time of the Middle Ages, did not become dogma until 1854 (from ‘Grace’). <br /><br />There was one part of a short story I found to be quite humorous (‘The Dead’): several Catholics are conversing with a Protestant. Mr. Browne, about monks who put people up who visit them at the monastery and do not charge room and board, and the kind of ascetic lifestyle the monks live: <br />He was astonished to hear that the monks never spoke, got up at two in the morning and slept in their coffins. He asked what they did it for.<br />—	“That’s the rule of the order,” said Aunt Katie firmly.<br />—	“Yes, but why?” asked Mr. Browne.<br />—	Aunt Katie repeated that it was the rule, that was all. Mr. Browne still seemed not to understand. Freddy Malins explained to him, as best he could, that the monks were trying to make up for the sins committed by all the sinners in the outside world, The explanation was not very clear for Mr. Browne grinned and said:<br />—	“I like that idea very much but wouldn’t a comfortable spring bed do them as well as a coffin?”<br /><br />One final observation from me and then I’ll shut my piehole. There were a number of stories in which people were alcoholic, or were drunk, or their family wished they would stay abstinent. In the majority of cases the alcoholism centered on male characters. (The cover illustration shows a man at a pub with a beer mug in his hand (‘Porter at the Fair’ by Jack B. Yeats, 1910). <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="9078722836928948966" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_896833678" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/896833678" />
    <a title="Agnieszka" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2446508-agnieszka"><img alt="Agnieszka" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1371835955p2/2446508.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/896833678?book_show_action=true">Mar 30, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Agnieszka" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Agnieszka" href="/user/show/2446508-agnieszka">Agnieszka</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/23291.Dubliners">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2446508-agnieszka?shelf=own-a-copy">own-a-copy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2446508-agnieszka?shelf=short-stories-and-novellas">short-stories-and-novellas</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2446508-agnieszka?shelf=2014">2014</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2446508-agnieszka?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer896833678" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3915391137024950745"><br/><i> Was no doubt about it: if you wanted to succeed you had to go away. You could do nothing in Dublin </i> .<br/><br/>The stories that make up <i> Dubliners </i> open with death and death ends it as well. And somewhere in between there is a life. The first truancy, the first timid amorous sighs and all shades of greyness, whole stretches of the usual humdrum reality. People caught up in the daily routine, whom life was withheld. <br/><br/>The workers, petty crooks and freeloaders, seamstresses, scullery maids, servants, scriven</span>
  <span id="freeText3915391137024950745" style="display:none"><br /><i> Was no doubt about it: if you wanted to succeed you had to go away. You could do nothing in Dublin </i> .<br /><br />The stories that make up <i> Dubliners </i> open with death and death ends it as well. And somewhere in between there is a life. The first truancy, the first timid amorous sighs and all shades of greyness, whole stretches of the usual humdrum reality. People caught up in the daily routine, whom life was withheld. <br /><br />The workers, petty crooks and freeloaders, seamstresses, scullery maids, servants, scriveners, salesmen, union activists - the whole cross-section of Irish middle and lower-middle class. Some of them crave for money, some for other places, some for love while others for another times. And the more they’re yearning the bigger is their disillusionment and discontent. Outcasts from life’s feast.<br /><br />Boy from <i> Araby </i>, enamoured of friend’s sister wants to visit a charity bazaar and buy something for the girl to find finally the bazaar closed, hero of <i> Counterparts </i> having pawned his watch, wants only to drink himself up but ends up with empty pockets and does not even feel drunk or Chandler, hero of <i> A little cloud </i>who’s eagerly awaiting his old friend to find him only vulgar and patronizing. People unfulfilled, for whom an intemperance is something as inevitable as climate changes, who take out all their failures, pathetic fate and frustration on children and weaker than themselves. Who feel that if they want to achieve anything in life they have to leave this town behind, that in Dublin actually there is no life.<br /><br />And so Joyce did. But no matter how much had he abandoned Dublin, after all he took this city with himself forever. He loved and hated it, became a bard of Dublin and its inhabitants, a great admirer but its stern critic at the same time. The same sentiments had he for his homeland, often in his works called <i> Errorland </i>. <br /><br />The main theme of <i> Dubliners </i> that ties together all stories is the breakdown of all values, embodied in drunkenness, decadent debauchery, obscurantism of clergy, hypocrisy, intellectual primitivism of bourgeoisie, and finally paralysis of the Irish political scene after the death of Parnell.<br /> <br />Joyce, chronicler of Dublin, alternately realistic and nostalgic, depicts city of lost hopes and failing chances to end this collection with absolutely brilliant story <i> The Dead </i> in which Gabriel counts on some pleasant moments with his wife, while she’s yearning for her dead lover, and finally falling snow reconciles everything, covering equally the living and the dead.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3915391137024950745" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_596155642" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/596155642" />
    <a title="Rakhi Dalal" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3655942-rakhi-dalal"><img alt="Rakhi Dalal" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1557596257p2/3655942.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/596155642?book_show_action=true">Apr 21, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rakhi Dalal" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rakhi Dalal" href="/user/show/3655942-rakhi-dalal">Rakhi Dalal</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Joyce fans</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3655942-rakhi-dalal?shelf=joyce">joyce</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3655942-rakhi-dalal?shelf=short-stories">short-stories</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer596155642" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3266613640530229482">Why do we wish to live this life; life, which at times seem to accompany the vague impressions we have long since been comfortable to carry along; the ideas, the choices, which have become a second nature to us. How many times do we stop and think about them? Particularly, as readers, as the ones who have been challenged, and hence in a way made aware by written word; how many times do we stop and think - life cannot always be a search, it cannot always be a constant exploration into unknown, a </span>
  <span id="freeText3266613640530229482" style="display:none">Why do we wish to live this life; life, which at times seem to accompany the vague impressions we have long since been comfortable to carry along; the ideas, the choices, which have become a second nature to us. How many times do we stop and think about them? Particularly, as readers, as the ones who have been challenged, and hence in a way made aware by written word; how many times do we stop and think - life cannot always be a search, it cannot always be a constant exploration into unknown, a desperate call to something which is striven for, for the attainment of something decisive. Or is it? Perhaps. But what when the decisive is attained, is conquered? Where does one go from there? Surely, in search of something still unknown, still unconquered! But we forget to stop in between. Or we rather choose to ignore <b>that</b> which comes in between, because we are too afraid to stop. And <b>that</b> is <b>life</b>. I remember this very beautiful quote by Allan Saunders:<br /><br /><i>“Life is what happens to you when you are busy making other plans.”</i><br /><br /> We forget that sometimes, life is also the acceptance of that which is presented to us by mere chances, or more than that, by the long witnessed “usual”. <br /><br />So, when I picked up <i>Dubliners</i>, while still continuing with <i>The Rebel</i>, I was at first annoyed because nothing seemed unusual or interesting there. But then, I just strove ahead because I had loved <i>“A Portrait of an Artist as a Young Man”</i> and so I wanted to give this a chance. Some more stories and I realized the simple idea with which these stories might have been penned. I realized that author might have wanted to portray life, as actually experienced and lived by the characters, who might in fact had been real people around him. People, who had lived a life, set by routine patterns and where nothing out of ordinary had ever happened. This realization made me sit straight and question myself. How many right ways can be there to live a life? One or two or more; Is it ours or theirs or still, somewhere between the two? I don’t even know if these are the right options. But what I do understand is that, either way it is life we are talking about. Life which is lived, both consciously and unconsciously, which may be different in living but which in the end culminates into the same. Oh, but by this I do not undermine one way or the other but simply wish to express the value of understanding both.<br /><br />It was the last story of the collection i.e. <i>“The Dead”</i> which deeply touched and gave me more food for thought. It actually brought to eyes something unusual from the rest of the stories :) [<a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[See, the incorrigible me! <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>] Gabriel, the protagonist of the story realized one day after a party that he didn’t know much about his wife Gretta, who seemed to have been in love with someone else all along. The story is not only about this awareness but also about love which gets shattered, even when the man in question has been long dead, and signifies the end of life as lived by Gabriel. <br /><br />The story ends with snow falling:<br /><br /><i>“His soul swooned slowly as he heard the snow falling faintly through the Universe and faintly falling, like the descent of their last end, upon all the living and the dead.”</i><br /><br />I do not doubt Joyce’s genius as a writer. After reading “Portrait” and few pages of “Ulysses”, this collection seemed just way too simplistic. But the thoughts it provoked after reading, is what makes it so readable. <br /><br />Definitely recommended.<br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="3266613640530229482" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span class='likeItContainer' id='like_container_review_596155642'><a id="like_count_review_596155642" rel="nofollow" href="/rating/voters/596155642?resource_type=Review"><span class="likesCount">78 likes</span></a>&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;<a rel="nofollow" class="jsLike like_it gr-button gr-button--small" href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/new?remember=true&amp;return_url=%2Fbook%2Fshow%2F11012.Dubliners">Like</a></span>

<div id="review_6817952" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/6817952" />
    <a title="Paul Bryant" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/416390-paul-bryant"><img alt="Paul Bryant" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1224113172p2/416390.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/6817952?book_show_action=true">Sep 26, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Paul Bryant" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Paul Bryant" href="/user/show/416390-paul-bryant">Paul Bryant</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/416390-paul-bryant?shelf=short-stories">short-stories</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/416390-paul-bryant?shelf=joyce">joyce</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer6817952" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13656317438090359181">For anyone thinking of putting James Joyce on your “must read this year” list for 2019 here are my suggestions.<br/><br/><b>BY</b><br/><br/>1.	<i>Dubliners</i><br/><br/>Brilliantly atmospheric scraps of Irish miserablism – must read to get where JJ is coming from. <br/><br/>2.	<i>A Portrait of the Artist as a Young Man</i><br/><br/>Strangely – tiresome and inessential. Bangs on about religion and more Irish miserablism and a bit too much like a portrait of the author as an insufferable young genius. <br/><br/>3.	<i>Ulysses</i><br/><br/>The essential book out of all of these. Difficult but al</span>
  <span id="freeText13656317438090359181" style="display:none">For anyone thinking of putting James Joyce on your “must read this year” list for 2019 here are my suggestions.<br><br><b>BY</b><br><br>1.	<i>Dubliners</i><br><br>Brilliantly atmospheric scraps of Irish miserablism – must read to get where JJ is coming from. <br><br>2.	<i>A Portrait of the Artist as a Young Man</i><br><br>Strangely – tiresome and inessential. Bangs on about religion and more Irish miserablism and a bit too much like a portrait of the author as an insufferable young genius. <br><br>3.	<i>Ulysses</i><br><br>The essential book out of all of these. Difficult but also very funny and not impossible. FWIW my short bluffer’s guide to this truly astonishing book is here<br><br><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/120808667">https://www.goodreads.com/review/show...</a><br><br>and my long review of it (chapter by chapter) is here<br><br><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/6752242">https://www.goodreads.com/review/show...</a><br><br>and here<br><br><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/story/show/280590-ulysses-review-part-2?chapter=0">https://www.goodreads.com/story/show/...</a><br><br>(it’s very long)<br><br>4.	<i>Finnegans Wake</i><br><br>This is really not recommended. But this is – a 10 minute excerpt (“Anna Livia Plurabelle”) read by JJ himself<br><br><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=grJC1yu4KRw">https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=grJC1...</a><br><br>I had thought, this small part being so beautiful, that FW would be another masterpiece, but the rest of it isn’t one tenth as fascinating or linguistically lovely, and it will do your brain in. The only thing I’ve been able to do with FW is parody it, rather lamely<br><br><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/99906161">https://www.goodreads.com/review/show...</a><br><br>which was unnervingly easy to do once you get into the swing of it. The reader who can gain enjoyment on any level from the great mapless madhouse that is FW has my undying respect. <br><br><b>ABOUT</b><br><br>1.	<i>The Most Dangerous Book : The Battle For James Joyce’s Ulysses</i> By Kevin Birmingham<br><br>By far the best book on JJ and Ulysses I ever read – you almost don’t need a real biography after this. It’s a total page turner. It’s not an analysis, it’s the story of how it was written and how it was published – long, painful and thrilling. <br><br>2.	<i>James Joyce</i> : Richard Ellman<br><br>But if you do want a big biography, this is the one. <br><br>3.	<i>My Brother’s Keeper</i> : Stanislaus Joyce<br><br>Or you could stick to this memoir by JJ’s faithful brother. It will make you love JJ (and Stanny) a lot more than most books will.<br><br>4.	<i>The New Bloomsday Book</i> : Harry Blamires<br><br>I liked this not-too-scholarly voyage round &amp; through &amp; about Ulysses better than any other analysis/interpretation<br><br>5.	<i>The Finnegans Wake Experience</i> : Roland McHugh<br><br>I only read one book about FW. It was this. It’s hilarious. Mr McHugh is a total obsessive with a screw loose &amp; dedicated his whole waking being to reading FW correctly and then explaining how to read FW correctly. Elastic bands are an important part of the process as I recall. I think it was self published so might be hard to track down. <br><br>6.	<i>James Joyce’s Odyssey</i> : Frank Delaney<br>7.	<i>James Joyce’s Dublin</i> : Edward Quinn<br><br>These two are luxury items - gorgeous photo books full of black &amp; white pix of dear dirty Dublin as it was and I don’t think is anymore. Not essential but just a delight. <br><br><b>AVOID</b><br><br>1.	<i>Ulysses and Us</i> : Declan Kiberd<br>2.	<i>Ulysses on the Liffey</i> : Richard Ellman<br><br>These two do exactly the same thing – with their jawbreakin pontificatin somnambulatin ramblin they like to make you want to find the English Literature department in your nearest university and burn it down. <br><br>3.	<i>Ulysses Annotated</i> : Don Gifford<br><br>Proving that the more you know the less you understand.<br><br><br>***<br><br><i>Cocklepickers. They waded a little way in the water and, stooping, soused their bags and, lifting them again, waded out. The dog yelped running to them, reared up and pawed them, dropping on all fours, again reared up at them with mute bearish fawning. Unheeded he kept by them as they came towards the drier sand, a rag of wolf’s tongue redpanting from his jaws. His speckled body ambled ahead of them and then loped off at a calf’s gallop. The carcass lay on his path. He stopped, sniffed, stalked round it, brother, nosing closer, went round it, sniffling rapidly like a dog all over the dead dog’s bedraggled fell. Dogskull, dogsniff, eyes on the ground, moves to one great goal. Ah, poor dogsbody! Here lies poor dogsbody’s body. <br>—Tatters! Out of that, you mongrel! </i><br><br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1543155807i/26646654.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><br>"How many roads must a man walk down...."<br><br>Sorry JJ, couldn't resist. </span>
  <a data-text-id="13656317438090359181" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_400767912" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/400767912" />
    <a title="David Schaafsma" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/269235-david-schaafsma"><img alt="David Schaafsma" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1574997804p2/269235.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/400767912?book_show_action=true">Oct 23, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David Schaafsma" class="user" itemprop="url" name="David Schaafsma" href="/user/show/269235-david-schaafsma">David Schaafsma</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/269235-david-schaafsma?shelf=best-books-ever">best-books-ever</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/269235-david-schaafsma?shelf=fiction-20th-century">fiction-20th-century</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer400767912" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12597748374661858950">“There was no doubt about it: if you wanted to succeed you had to go away. You could do nothing in Dublin”--Joyce <br/><br/>"Every night as I gazed up at the window I said softly to myself the word paralysis. It had always sounded strangely in my ears, like the word gnomon in the Euclid and the word simony in the Catechism. But now it sounded to me like the name of some maleficent and sinful being. It filled me with fear, and yet I longed to be nearer to it and to look upon its deadly work.” <br/><br/>Dubliners is,</span>
  <span id="freeText12597748374661858950" style="display:none">“There was no doubt about it: if you wanted to succeed you had to go away. You could do nothing in Dublin”--Joyce <br /><br />"Every night as I gazed up at the window I said softly to myself the word paralysis. It had always sounded strangely in my ears, like the word gnomon in the Euclid and the word simony in the Catechism. But now it sounded to me like the name of some maleficent and sinful being. It filled me with fear, and yet I longed to be nearer to it and to look upon its deadly work.” <br /><br />Dubliners is, by reputation (among English professors and scholars, at least) one of the greatest collections of short stories ever produced. Of course, as they say, them’s fightin’ words, so have it your own way, but I vote with that crowd of high admirers, and always have, having read it or stories from it, many times. This is the first time I am hearing it read aloud, in the appropriately Irish voice of Connor Sheridan, that somehow captures the dry and at sometimes mournful wit the ex-patriate Joyce brings to this tribute to the Dubliners he left behind. Some have found it maudlin, even grim, primarily a critique of the people Joyce left behind, but I found it at turns gently satirical, sometimes melancholy, and always loving, portraits of a time and place, filled with local politics and religion and (especially) finely sketched characters, some stories focused on lost opportunities for love or leaving. <br /><br />In 2000 Time Magazine listed the greatest novels of the twentieth century and listed the difficult English major Everest of Ulysses as the worthiest literary mountain to climb, #1, which prompted thousands of Americans who may never have read 100 novels to read the first three pages and promptly declare Joyce a boring and inscrutable idiot. Though I do think Ulysses is one of the greatest novels ever written, I don’t think it would be particularly enjoyable for the general population; nor do I think most people “should” read it. But Joyce is an amazing writer; he wrote four works of fiction, in increasing levels of difficulty and formal experimentalism. If you like short stories and want to try Joyce I would try Dubliners, the most recognizably traditional stories he wrote. If you like that, I might then try the somewhat more formally challenging A Portrait of the Artist as a Young Man. If you decide to go to graduate school, then consider Ulysses, sure, but only then, which owes something mock-epic to Homer’s Odyssey, and each chapter in a literary style of different periods/centuries. Finnegan’s Wake, which took him twenty years to write, almost no one reads, for good reason. It is so experimental most people can’t make heads or tails of a single paragraph. (No, I have not yet finished it, and probably never will).<br /><br />Dubliners, published in 1914 (after nearly ten years of his trying to get it published!), is short, as story collections go. I have my favorites: “Eveline,” about a young shop girl conflicted about leaving her widowed father to live life with a sailor:<br /><br />“He rushed beyond the barrier and called to her to follow. She set her white face to him, passive, like a helpless animal. Her eyes gave him no sign of love or farewell or recognition.” <br /> <br />And “Araby,” about a shy young man’s fruitless pursuit of a young woman, dooming them both to loneliness.<br /><br />“. . . and yet her name was like a summons to all my foolish blood.” <br /><br />“Sometimes he caught himself listening to the sound of his own voice. He thought that in her eyes he would ascent to an angelical stature; and, as he attached the fervent nature of his companion more and more closely to him, he heard the strange impersonal voice which he recognised as his own, insisting on the soul's incurable loneliness. We cannot give ourselves, it said: we are our own.” <br /><br />He’s ambitious for her, but at the same time, he sees himself clearly and sadly: “Gazing up into the darkness I saw myself as a creature driven and derided by vanity; and my eyes burned with anguish and anger.”<br /><br />Some of the deft observations of character in the writing are beautiful. Of one woman: “She respected her husband in the same way as she respected the General Post Office, as something large, secure and fixed: and though she knew the small number of his talents she appreciated his abstract value as a male.” <br /><br />And about Mr. Duffy: “He lived at a little distance from his body, regarding his own acts with doubtful side-glances. He had an odd autobiographical habit which led him to compose in his mind from time to time a short sentence about himself containing a subject in the third person and a verb in the past tense.” <br /><br />The true gem of the collection may be the magnificent and mournful closing long story, “The Dead,” which features Gabriel, asked to give a short speech in honor of his aunts at a holiday party, who is disappointed not to “experience intimacy” with his wife Greta after the party, seeing her sadly draped on the bed. A song that was sung at the party reminded her of a time when she was seventeen when she had loved a boy, Michael Furey, who lost his life in the war. Gabriel is jealous of a love she sees Greta had for this boy, a love that he and Greta have perhaps never had themselves. And then, this reflection, using snow to punctuate Gabriel's sense of himself and maybe Joyce's view of Dublin:<br /><br />“A few light taps upon the pane made him turn to the window. It had begun to snow again. He watched sleepily the flakes, silver and dark, falling obliquely against the lamplight. The time had come for him to set out on his journey westward. Yes, the newspapers were right: snow was general all over Ireland. It was falling on every part of the dark central plain, on the treeless hills, falling softly upon the Bog of Allen and, farther westward, softly falling into the dark mutinous Shannon waves. It was falling, too, upon every part of the lonely churchyard on the hill where Michael Furey lay buried. It lay thickly drifted on the crooked crosses and headstones, on the spears of the little gate, on the barren thorns. His soul swooned slowly as he heard the snow falling faintly through the universe and faintly falling, like the descent of their last end, upon all the living and the dead.”<br /><br />Proust wrote: "In reality, when he reads, each reader is actually the reader of his own self. The work of the writer is nothing more than a kind of optical instrument that the writer offers. It allows the reader to discern that which, without the book, he might not have been able to see in himself."<br /><br />Do we not in our empathetic reading of Gabriel, see ourselves and reflect on our own lives?<br /><br />Many characters in Dubliners experience the struggle about whether to stay or leave, or to just act passionately, facing a kind of paralysis that Joyce refers to in the opening story, “The Sisters”:<br /><br />“I wanted real adventures to happen to myself. But real adventures, I reflected, do not happen to people who remain at home: they must be sought abroad.” One must act, one must move, one must engage with the world, one must break free from provincial beliefs. <br /><br />Dubliners is a wonderful collection, short enough to read in a few hours. It’s full of self-reflection and "inwardness." Listen to it, read it. Some of the stories have been made into films, like John Huston’s The Dead.<br /><br />Here’s the whole story “The Dead” for you to read. (You’re welcome):<br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://english-learners.com/wp-content/uploads/THE-DEAD.pdf">http://english-learners.com/wp-conten...</a><br /> <br /> <br />.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12597748374661858950" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
