<div id="review_281984980" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/281984980" />
    <a title="Ted" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7213075-ted"><img alt="Ted" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1553101210p2/7213075.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/281984980?book_show_action=true">Feb 22, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ted" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ted" href="/user/show/7213075-ted">Ted</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780140186475" href="/book/show/764450.Dubliners">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7213075-ted?shelf=short-stories">short-stories</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7213075-ted?shelf=read-in-20s">read-in-20s</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7213075-ted?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7213075-ted?shelf=cities">cities</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7213075-ted?shelf=lit-irish">lit-irish</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7213075-ted?shelf=have">have</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7213075-ted?shelf=reviews-most-liked">reviews-most-liked</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7213075-ted?shelf=multiple-reads">multiple-reads</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7213075-ted?shelf=beach-mixed">beach-mixed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7213075-ted?shelf=place">place</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer281984980" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3280972892662280551"><b>review update – 5/15/17</b><br/><br/>The first twelve stories of <i>Dubliners</i> were submitted to a publisher in 1905, when Joyce was 22. They were accepted, but squeamishness on the publisher’s part kept delaying publication. Over the next three years Joyce submitted three additional stories. Finally he took the collection to a second publisher. Again it was accepted, and again it was held back. Finally, in 1914, the original publisher overcame his fears and released the volume to the public. <br/><br/>By now, however, Joy</span>
  <span id="freeText3280972892662280551" style="display:none"><b>review update – 5/15/17</b><br /><br />The first twelve stories of <i>Dubliners</i> were submitted to a publisher in 1905, when Joyce was 22. They were accepted, but squeamishness on the publisher’s part kept delaying publication. Over the next three years Joyce submitted three additional stories. Finally he took the collection to a second publisher. Again it was accepted, and again it was held back. Finally, in 1914, the original publisher overcame his fears and released the volume to the public. <br /><br />By now, however, Joyce’s <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7588.A_Portrait_of_the_Artist_as_a_Young_Man" title="A Portrait of the Artist as a Young Man by James Joyce" rel="nofollow noopener">Portrait of the Artist</a> was appearing in a serialized version, and the novel overshadowed the short stories; as did, or course, Joyce’s two modern masterpieces, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/338798.Ulysses" title="Ulysses by James Joyce" rel="nofollow noopener">Ulysses</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/11013.Finnegans_Wake" title="Finnegans Wake by James Joyce" rel="nofollow noopener">Finnegan’s Wake</a>.<br /><br />So it was only slowly, over the course of many years, that <i>Dubliners</i> gained recognition for both the modernism and the rather brute realism of its stories. <br /><br />In the previous update, I chose to use the word <i>dreary</i> in describing the stories. That they are. But this time let’s try “resignation” – stories of resignation. This is perhaps better, since it’s less ambiguous. Joyce writes about the people of Dublin as resigned to the lives they have – controlled by the Church (to some extent), controlled by the British (to the extent the British give a damn), prey to the simple pleasures of drink, having children, and pretending that life’s not really so bad. And of course there are classes in this society, so that those of any class except the bottom can always compare themselves pridefully to those below them, should they care to.<br /><br />I scanned through the last, longest story (The Dead), looking for a good quotation. Alas, they were few and far between, and too long to bother with. But this story is a fit capstone to the collection. It’s about a traditional New Year’s Eve celebration that a few dozen of Dublin’s better off citizens partake of, an evening of music, dancing, feasting. Nothing about anyone’s death, though the protagonist, Gabriel, has rather morose thoughts often during the evening.<br /><br />Then in the last few pages, a tale of death finds its way into the story, a death that occurred long ago, but is newly revealed to Gabriel and causes him to have very quotable thoughts as he falls asleep. But, it occurred to me that the story’s title refers not just to these last few pages, but to all the people celebrating that evening - Joyce suggesting that even these well fed, happy people, in failing to recognize the resignation with which they accept their lives, are in their own way, though “living”, part of The Dead.<br /><br /><br /><b>review update - 3/17/15</b><br /><br /><i>obviously in celebration of a certain day </i><br /><br />Just a few thoughts on these stories a couple years later.<br /><br />When I said below that the stories aren't "exciting" ... yes, well, first I didn't mean that they were not very affecting stories, because some of them are. One could use the word "depressing"? But more, I think the atmosphere of the stories is probably much like the weather that I associate with the Emerald Isle. Damp, cloudy, hints of rain, chill in most parts of the year, maybe summerlike for a couple weeks in July. Gloomy. Weather that makes you seek out a pub and the warm comfort of a pint with friends. Then there's that Catholic haze that looms over everything, the haze and the weather and maybe even the people such that Joyce himself had to flee.<br /><br />Whenever you feel like subjecting yourself to this sort of dreariness, which should be <i>often</i>, read one of the stories, it will suit your yearning.<br /><br /><br /><b>original review</b><br /><br />These aren't the most exciting short stories ever written. They were written by Joyce, though, so that sets them on a level of Literature that most writers can only dream of. It also means that they are worthy of study, and that the time spent studying them will be well spent.<br /><br />Terence Brown's Introduction shows that he has studied these stories for a long time, and his Notes make it apparent that there is not a word, a slang term, a Dublin location, nor a historical reference in the stories that he does not know most everything about. (The footnoting is at times a bit distracting - "of course, everyone knows that" you think - but of course those things that "everyone knows" vary from reader to reader.) All in all, this is a very good edition of <i>Dubliners</i>.<br /><br />I was once an English lit. major in college (only for a year), and still have infrequent yearnings in that direction. One of those I have had in recent years is to take the time to write a long essay on these stories. I do think they are worth that kind of effort.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3280972892662280551" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer6133548546836628542">Reading this book is like meeting a perfect stranger at the park. The two of you sitting on a bench, they sharing their truth with you, you sharing yours with them. Just a short, yet meaningful interaction. Something with no responsibilities and no strings attached. And then, at some point, “oh, it’s two o’ clock already, I’d better be going”. And that was it. One could argue that that’s the case with all interactions in one’s life.<br/>Joyce offers us a synthesis of people and their actions, their f</span>
  <span id="freeText6133548546836628542" style="display:none">Reading this book is like meeting a perfect stranger at the park. The two of you sitting on a bench, they sharing their truth with you, you sharing yours with them. Just a short, yet meaningful interaction. Something with no responsibilities and no strings attached. And then, at some point, “oh, it’s two o’ clock already, I’d better be going”. And that was it. One could argue that that’s the case with all interactions in one’s life.<br />Joyce offers us a synthesis of people and their actions, their fears, their misconceptions, their loves and their hates. Brief zoom-ins into the details that make up Dublin of his time. I kind of lost myself there, drawing conclusions and finding meanings in symbolisms that could very well be anything but symbolisms. I think that’s why many people didn’t like <i>Dubliners</i>, and I think it's the same reason I did. Thought-provoking isn’t it?</span>

<div id="review_1069028864" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1069028864" />
    <a title="Renato Magalhães Rocha" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/29402621-renato-magalh-es-rocha"><img alt="Renato Magalhães Rocha" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1524097330p2/29402621.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1069028864?book_show_action=true">Oct 01, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Renato Magalhães Rocha" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Renato Magalhães Rocha" href="/user/show/29402621-renato-magalh-es-rocha">Renato Magalhães Rocha</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/23293623-dublinenses">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/29402621-renato-magalh-es-rocha?shelf=quickies">quickies</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/29402621-renato-magalh-es-rocha?shelf=kindle-ebooks">kindle-ebooks</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/29402621-renato-magalh-es-rocha?shelf=joyce">joyce</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/29402621-renato-magalh-es-rocha?shelf=short-stories">short-stories</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/29402621-renato-magalh-es-rocha?shelf=2014">2014</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/29402621-renato-magalh-es-rocha?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1069028864" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11477631136317960275">My relationship with <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/5144.James_Joyce' title='James Joyce' rel='nofollow noopener'>James Joyce</a> has started off well and I'm excited to take on the next step: I've been wanting to read <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/338798.Ulysses' title='Ulysses by James Joyce' rel='nofollow noopener'>Ulysses</a> for quite some time, and after finishing <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1381.The_Odyssey' title='The Odyssey by Homer' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Odyssey</a>, I figured I'd read <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/11012.Dubliners' title='Dubliners by James Joyce' rel='nofollow noopener'>Dubliners</a> as some of the characters in his short stories appear in minor roles on his longer, modernist novel.<br/><br/>This is a collection of fifteen short stories - and I'll keep this a short review as well - that deals with the Irish middle class life in and around Dublin in the beginning of the 1900's</span>
  <span id="freeText11477631136317960275" style="display:none">My relationship with <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/5144.James_Joyce" title="James Joyce" rel="nofollow noopener">James Joyce</a> has started off well and I'm excited to take on the next step: I've been wanting to read <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/338798.Ulysses" title="Ulysses by James Joyce" rel="nofollow noopener">Ulysses</a> for quite some time, and after finishing <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1381.The_Odyssey" title="The Odyssey by Homer" rel="nofollow noopener">The Odyssey</a>, I figured I'd read <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/11012.Dubliners" title="Dubliners by James Joyce" rel="nofollow noopener">Dubliners</a> as some of the characters in his short stories appear in minor roles on his longer, modernist novel.<br /><br />This is a collection of fifteen short stories - and I'll keep this a short review as well - that deals with the Irish middle class life in and around Dublin in the beginning of the 1900's. In less than 200 pages, Joyce depicts love, violence, routine, longing for escape, religion and epiphany. His stories are not packed with action per se, but they subtly have a lot to tell.<br /><br />As much as I would like to, I can't say that I am a very visual person as far as my reading goes. I understand what's going on and feel the sensations and emotions the writer is portraying, but I rarely imagine the visuals being described. This is something I'm trying to work on, but I'm not forcing myself either to change how I perceive a book. However, Joyce has accomplished what only few other writers do: he aroused my visual sense to new levels. While I was reading his stories, there were short movies going on in my head without me even trying it, and I was able to connect them with some of the best movies I've ever watched.<br /><br />The second story, <i>An Encounter</i>, about a group of schoolboys playing "cowboy and Indian battles" gave me Truffaut's <a href="http://www.imdb.com/title/tt0053198/" rel="nofollow noopener">Les quatre cents coups</a> vibes with a touch of Yves Robert's <a href="http://www.imdb.com/title/tt0054949/" rel="nofollow noopener">La guerre des boutons</a>. I was impressed by how much Joyce was able to adapt his language and style to that of a child narrator with so much maestry.<br /><br />Another movie memory that <i>Dubliners</i> evoked in me was David Lean's <a href="http://www.imdb.com/title/tt0037558/" rel="nofollow noopener">Brief Encounter</a> with his story <i>A Painful Case</i> about a man and a woman that meet, fall in love but can't be together as she's already married. This story deals with loneliness that we all at some point in our lives have felt at least for some moments, and Joyce's words quoted below gave me chills as I read and re-read them repeatedly:<br /><blockquote>
  <i>"He looked down the slope and, at the base, in the shadow of the wall of the Park, he saw some human figures lying. Those venal and furtive loves filled him with despair. He gnawed the rectitude of his life; he felt that he had been outcast from life's feast."</i>
</blockquote><br />But as much as there are great qualities in all of the stories, <i>The Dead</i> tops them all. Joyce's development of this story and the themes explored really spoke to me. The attachment to the past felt by Gabriel and the ache he felt for not having loved his wife - or any woman - so deeply were as easy to feel as when you're watching one of your closest friends in pain. That's how well written it was for me.<br /><br /><i>The Dead</i> also has one of the best conclusions of any story - long or short - I've read. As Gabriel numbly gazed out the window, he contemplates about life and death and how we're all - the living and the dead - still connected as finding out about someone who has passed away for several years still brought up some emotions Gabriel has been concealing. I can't avoid quoting it here to try and borrow to my review some of the brilliance with which Joyce ended his book:<br /><blockquote>
  <i>"A few light taps upon the pane made him turn to the window. It had begun to snow again. He watched sleepily the flakes, silver and dark, falling obliquely against the lamplight. The time had come for him to set out on his journey westward. Yes, the newspapers were right: snow was general all over Ireland. It was falling on every part of the dark central plain, on the treeless hills, falling softly upon the Bog of Allen and, farther westward, softly falling into the dark mutinous Shannon waves. It was falling, too, upon every part of the lonely churchyard on the hill where Michael Furey lay buried. It lay thickly drifted on the crooked crosses and headstones, on the spears of the little gate, on the barren thorns. His soul swooned slowly as he heard the snow falling faintly through the universe and faintly falling, like the descent of their last end, upon all the living and the dead."</i>
</blockquote><br />Rating: 4 stars</span>
  <a data-text-id="11477631136317960275" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_161378914" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/161378914" />
    <a title="Rajat Ubhaykar" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4686064-rajat-ubhaykar"><img alt="Rajat Ubhaykar" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1352663955p2/4686064.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/161378914?book_show_action=true">Apr 15, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rajat Ubhaykar" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rajat Ubhaykar" href="/user/show/4686064-rajat-ubhaykar">Rajat Ubhaykar</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/1100580.Dubliners">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4686064-rajat-ubhaykar?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer161378914" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11765391874548148715"><i>"For myself, I always write about Dublin, because if I can get to the heart of Dublin I can get to the heart of all the cities of the world. In the particular is contained the universal."</i><br/>-James Joyce<br/><br/>Dubliners is fantastic literary inspiration, it forced me to take better notice of my surroundings, of my own city, which has an untapped endless source of heartbreak, joy, turmoil and everything else to do with the human predicament. It also almost forced me to park myself anywhere and write somethi</span>
  <span id="freeText11765391874548148715" style="display:none"><i>"For myself, I always write about Dublin, because if I can get to the heart of Dublin I can get to the heart of all the cities of the world. In the particular is contained the universal."</i><br />-James Joyce<br /><br />Dubliners is fantastic literary inspiration, it forced me to take better notice of my surroundings, of my own city, which has an untapped endless source of heartbreak, joy, turmoil and everything else to do with the human predicament. It also almost forced me to park myself anywhere and write something worthwhile, but that's another story, I hope, someday.<br /> <br />What do I think of Joyce? The man's a genius, undoubtedly. He does what he set out to do masterfully. He lays Dublin bare. His writing is powerful, unassuming and devoid of judgment. It can often be emotionally draining and occasionally soul-crushing to read his stories if you manage to get into them, which can be a demanding task considering the colloquial language and the quotidian, sparse, yet very representative plot lines. It is awe-inspiring to watch him lay out the intricacies of character interplay mainly through authentic dialogue. <br /><br />The protagonists age as the book progresses, so while the first story is from the point of view of a seven year old child, the final story is The Dead, recognizably about death and old age, his most famous short-story. Through these characters belonging to different backgrounds and age groups, he paints a realistic, stark picture of Dublin. There are also stories which are first-person narratives, where he gets under the skin of the characters inhumanly well, 'A Painful Case' being an apt example and my favourite story.<br /> <br />Everything said, a necessary addition to any book-lover's collection.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11765391874548148715" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2211045020" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2211045020" />
    <a title="Steven Godin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/26706841-steven-godin"><img alt="Steven Godin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1605181037p2/26706841.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2211045020?book_show_action=true">Aug 30, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Steven Godin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Steven Godin" href="/user/show/26706841-steven-godin">Steven Godin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26706841-steven-godin?shelf=classic-fiction">classic-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26706841-steven-godin?shelf=short-stories">short-stories</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26706841-steven-godin?shelf=ireland">ireland</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2211045020" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16958851060524471400">Dubliners is one of those books that simply tracks life. Joyce had written most of these stories by the age of twenty-three, he did so with the understanding and forbearance of someone much older. He often portrayed himself as sitting in judgment on his fellow Dubliners, whom he once described to a friend as the most hopeless, useless and inconsistent race of charlatans I have ever come across. Am sure he didn't mean it. What gives the stories their tremendous power is precisely their refusal to</span>
  <span id="freeText16958851060524471400" style="display:none">Dubliners is one of those books that simply tracks life. Joyce had written most of these stories by the age of twenty-three, he did so with the understanding and forbearance of someone much older. He often portrayed himself as sitting in judgment on his fellow Dubliners, whom he once described to a friend as the most hopeless, useless and inconsistent race of charlatans I have ever come across. Am sure he didn't mean it. What gives the stories their tremendous power is precisely their refusal to make judgements. The men and women depicted in this collection are mostly a shabby bunch: drunkards, wife-beaters, narcissists, hypocrites. But Joyce is careful to show the forces that have made them who they are, the exigencies that constrict them, the disillusionments that have sapped their will to act differently from others. He believed that by showing us ourselves, he could help us understand each other better, forgive each other more often, and break out of our holding patterns and begin to change. He believed that redemption was something we could achieve for ourselves. Taking in the aspirations of the people in the city we see what they wish for, and what they envisage for their offspring. In all then on the surface a deceptively easy book to read, but think deeper and this becomes something that not only can give plenty of pleasurable reading, but also a fascinating time if you really wish analyse the finer details in each tale. They appear here very much in the correct order as we progress through the stages of life, and this is very fulfilling.<br /><br />'The dead' is one of the finest short-stories I have ever read, and it's saved until last. Some other highlights for me were - 'Eveline', 'Two Gallants' 'A Little Cloud' and 'A Mother'.<br />The reason for not giving five stars (even though 'The Dead' is easily worthy of that on it's own) is simply down to fact some stories were better than others. </span>
  <a data-text-id="16958851060524471400" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1031243240" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1031243240" />
    <a title="Cheryl" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/11255849-cheryl"><img alt="Cheryl" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1565547225p2/11255849.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1031243240?book_show_action=true">Nov 20, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Cheryl" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Cheryl" href="/user/show/11255849-cheryl">Cheryl</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/368917.Dubliners">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11255849-cheryl?shelf=short-shorts">short-shorts</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11255849-cheryl?shelf=vintage">vintage</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1031243240" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13041214764997575313"><i>Dubliners</i> is a good collection to read on a quiet Sunday evening, if only to disappear from the rest of the world and into Joyce's version of Dublin, Ireland. It's also a good feeling to delve into a book that was accepted for publication in 1904, and yet, "due to puritan prudery, it got passed from fearful publisher to fearful publisher" until someone had the good sense to publish it nine years later. Thank you for the publication and for reiterating Joyce's reasons of isolation from Victorian </span>
  <span id="freeText13041214764997575313" style="display:none"><i>Dubliners</i> is a good collection to read on a quiet Sunday evening, if only to disappear from the rest of the world and into Joyce's version of Dublin, Ireland. It's also a good feeling to delve into a book that was accepted for publication in 1904, and yet, "due to puritan prudery, it got passed from fearful publisher to fearful publisher" until someone had the good sense to publish it nine years later. Thank you for the publication and for reiterating Joyce's reasons of isolation from Victorian society; perhaps this is why he understands the "outsider" narrative so deeply. <br /><br />When I taught a College Program at a rural high school, I found Joyce's short stories easy to teach because not only do they have the layered and crisp writing a student at that level digests easily, but a few of the stories also deal with the theme of choice, which makes for great lecture discussions. Take <b>"Eveline"</b> for example, where a young woman must choose whether to leave her drunken and abusive father by escaping with her sailor fiancee, or to abide by the promise she made to her dying mother: to stay home and take care of the home; notwithstanding the idea she'd found her mother "pitiful" to have led such a life. Imagine the discussions, ponders, and distilling essays that arose from such a story.<br /><br />So I decided to revisit this collection of fifteen stories, each written with the ordinary life in mind, each a reminder of the choices of love, family, and career; each an encapsulation of loneliness and emotional and spiritual awakening. You don't get the same writing style of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7588.A_Portrait_of_the_Artist_as_a_Young_Man" title="A Portrait of the Artist as a Young Man by James Joyce" rel="nofollow noopener">A Portrait of the Artist as a Young Man</a>, but you get the same thematic undertones. And somehow, you don't read a Joyce book without finding yourself engulfed in moments of reflection. <br /> <br />In <b>"Little Cloud," </b> there is the struggle with parallels, as a main character sees his friend's poetic success as his measurement of success and this leaves him disillusioned as he<br /><blockquote>watched the scene and thought of life; and (as always happened when he thought of life) he became sad. A gentle melancholy took possession of him. He felt how useless it was to struggle against fortune, this being the burden of wisdom which the ages had bequeathed to him.</blockquote><br />And just as one wonders whether the character in <b>"Little Cloud"</b> accepts his life as a father and husband, or whether he fails at it in his pursuit of a poetry career, one wonders about the characters in <b>"The Boarding House"</b> because this is how Joyce ends his stories: inconclusively. You read, you decide. The characters in "Boarding House" are young and in love, but their society dictates that after their brief affair, marriage should be inevitable. But is he ready for marriage like she is? <br /><blockquote>She <i>was</i> a little vulgar; sometimes she said <i>I seen</i> and <i>If I had've known</i>. But what would grammar matter if he really loved her? He could not make up his mind whether to like her or despise her for what she had done. Of course, he had done it too. His instinct urged him to remain free, not to marry. Once you are married you are done for, it said.</blockquote><br />According to the editor of this collection, Joyce left Ireland with feelings of "rage, resentment and revenge;"I would also add, disdain of spiritual shackles. Some of these feelings are also embedded within these stories, as in <b>"The Sisters"</b> and <b>"An Encounter"</b>. But just as he highlights the torment of conformity, in some small way, he also indicates the beauty of individual thinking. </span>
  <a data-text-id="13041214764997575313" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer4145307140781723244">Dear James Joyce, <br/><br/>So let's pretend you might actually receive this letter. I just experienced your short story collection. Maybe it wasn't the best choice for taking a first time walk into your imagination. <br/><br/>I just don't get you, man. What makes you tick?   <span id="freeText4145307140781723244" style="display:none">Dear James Joyce, <br /><br />So let's pretend you might actually receive this letter. I just experienced your short story collection. Maybe it wasn't the best choice for taking a first time walk into your imagination. <br /><br />I just don't get you, man. What makes you tick? What message are you hoping that someone reading will feel right into their soul? I wanted to love Dublin like YOU love Dublin. But just nothing happened. <br /><br />Because great literary men have come before you- Hardy, Tolstoy,Dostoyevsky, Steinbeck, Dumas, Hugo, Fitzgerald..just to name a few. All of them at one time or another have rocked my little world. But your words did not capture my heart, my mind or my soul. What did I miss that so many others were able to see? <br /><br />The thing is...I'm just not that into you. <br /><br />Sincerely, <br /><br />Erin<br /><br />P.S. It's too bad it didn't work out, I would have enjoyed referring to you as J.J. <br /><br /><br /><br />Goodreads review published 14/08/19</span>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer1037449210" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer14613329526242220726">I find <i>Dubliners</i> to be a perfect example of the love-hate relationship that James Joyce had with his native city. <br/>On the negative side, there is his choice of (mostly) mean, depressing subject matter. On the positive side, there is the writing itself - pristine and done with loving care.<br/>In the end, at least for me, love wins out.<br/><br/>As anyone who's read <i>Dubliners</i> knows, "The Dead" is a masterpiece. <br/>Last year, the Irish Repertory Theatre did a theatrical production of "The Dead" at the American Irish</span>
  <span id="freeText14613329526242220726" style="display:none">I find <i>Dubliners</i> to be a perfect example of the love-hate relationship that James Joyce had with his native city. <br />On the negative side, there is his choice of (mostly) mean, depressing subject matter. On the positive side, there is the writing itself - pristine and done with loving care.<br />In the end, at least for me, love wins out.<br /><br />As anyone who's read <i>Dubliners</i> knows, "The Dead" is a masterpiece. <br />Last year, the Irish Repertory Theatre did a theatrical production of "The Dead" at the American Irish Historical Society in Manhattan with a sumptuous dinner served for (and by some of) the actors and the audience. Performances were sold out by the time I read about it, but I can at least imagine what the production and the dinner party must have been like.<br />Here's a review - <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2016/12/08/theater/the-dead-1904-review.html">https://www.nytimes.com/2016/12/08/th...</a><br /><br />There were obvious unresolved problems in transforming the final scene into theatre, but I would have liked to have seen that production anyway - and to have eaten that meal - just reading about it in the book and in the review made my mouth water.<br /><br />Some of my soundtrack and intermission music while reading:<br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=bRBpYtPBnOo">https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=bRBpY...</a><br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=Xtxc87oDYZ8">https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=Xtxc8...</a><br /><br />Laura's review: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2602150786?book_show_action=false&amp;from_review_page=1">https://www.goodreads.com/review/show...</a> made me want to add one more to my soundtrack:<br />Dick Gaughan's version of Patrick Kavanagh's poem "Raglan Road"<br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=gRVWX0TsBaY">https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=gRVWX...</a></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer9337690315287802465">As powerful a commitment to the form to be found in English. The original fourteen stories should be read as a <i>set piece</i>: as they portray the evolution of thought from childhood to adulthood: from dogmatic belief to reasoned denial. <i>The Dead</i> should be viewed separately. Five-stars!</span>

          </span>

<div id="review_2346828568" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2346828568" />
    <a title="P.E." class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/68429005-p-e"><img alt="P.E." src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1543802303p2/68429005.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2346828568?book_show_action=true">Mar 27, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="P.E." class="user" itemprop="url" name="P.E." href="/user/show/68429005-p-e">P.E.</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/23291.Dubliners">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/68429005-p-e?shelf=irish-literature">irish-literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/68429005-p-e?shelf=scathing">scathing</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/68429005-p-e?shelf=black-humour">black-humour</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/68429005-p-e?shelf=absurd">absurd</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/68429005-p-e?shelf=ideology">ideology</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/68429005-p-e?shelf=macabre">macabre</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/68429005-p-e?shelf=short-stories">short-stories</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/68429005-p-e?shelf=urban-literature">urban-literature</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2346828568" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3509799610816815219">A feeling, uncompromising and superb depiction of low middle-class life in Dublin at the turn of the 20th century. I am particularly impressed by how the short stories are echoing one another.<br/><br/><i>Tremendously</i> helpful introduction and notes by Trinity College Professor Terence Brown.<br/><br/><br/><b>My favourite stories :</b><br/><br/>The Sisters *<br/>An Encounter<br/>Araby *<br/>Eveline<br/>After the Race<br/>Two Gallants *<br/>The Boarding House *<br/>A Little Cloud *<br/>Counterparts *<br/>Clay<br/>A Painful Case *<br/>Ivy Day in the Committee Room<br/>A Mother<br/>Grace<br/>The Dead *<br/><br/><br/><b>RELATED RE</b></span>
  <span id="freeText3509799610816815219" style="display:none">A feeling, uncompromising and superb depiction of low middle-class life in Dublin at the turn of the 20th century. <span id="freeTextContainer7802722711007014378">I must confess I dreaded a little to start reading something of James Joyce. I think I made the wright choice to start with 'Dubliners'. I really appreciated the stories although they are not always easy to understand. The last story for example begins with festivities for Christmas. At the end of the party the woman of the main charachter introduces herself. She descends from the staircase as in many ghoststories the ghost appears.   <span id="freeText7802722711007014378" style="display:none">I must confess I dreaded a little to start reading something of James Joyce. I think I made the wright choice to start with 'Dubliners'. I really appreciated the stories although they are not always easy to understand. The last story for example begins with festivities for Christmas. At the end of the party the woman of the main charachter introduces herself. She descends from the staircase as in many ghoststories the ghost appears. One wonders if it's a ghost, if she's just an image that Gabriel sees. From than on it is a story of the couple and the bound between them. Joyce makes any effort to accentuate death. Is the woman then dead as we first tought? Also Gabriel is obsessed by death<br /> More exactly the death of former boyfriends of his wife. In the end they are in bed and he asks himself:<br /><br />"One by one they where all becoming shapes. Better pass boldly into that other world , in the full glory of some passion, than fade and wither dismally with age. He thought of how she who lay beside him had locked in her heart for so many years that image of her lover's eyes when he had told her that he did not wish to live."<br /><br />There is much passion in this tale, joy in the beginning and jealousy and death at the end. <br /><br />The stories also end with this story and with death. Also in the first story we see death as a priest lays on his deathbed. A young boy was his friend and can not accept he is death. Though he is confronted with truth and learns about death. <br /><br />You could say the stories form a cycle: they begin and end with death. Further in the other stories we see a whole kind of different persons: how they live, what they do and what they say. Most people are poor and/or working class. So no luxuries for them . Their life is basal and although it is, they mostly enjoy it. It gives an important picture of live in Dublin around 1900 .</span>

<div id="review_115740720" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/115740720" />
    <a title="Robin Tell-Drake" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2073647-robin-tell-drake"><img alt="Robin Tell-Drake" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1409704752p2/2073647.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/115740720?book_show_action=true">Aug 07, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Robin Tell-Drake" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Robin Tell-Drake" href="/user/show/2073647-robin-tell-drake">Robin Tell-Drake</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780670285860" href="/book/show/742363.Dubliners">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2073647-robin-tell-drake?shelf=literary">literary</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer115740720" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14677740153189811010">I suppose I've always intended to read Joyce; it's terribly daunting but seems inevitable, too, that I must follow the man all the way through to <i>Finnegans Wake</i>. I have a copy. Untouched. Another remnant of the days when I thought I was on Earth to prove some kind of a point.<br/><br/>But I'm still awfully curious, and this year I finally dipped a toe in. <i>Dubliners</i> came first and seemed easiest to start with, and I'd read a story or two of it already. And indeed it is pretty conventional, even self-consci</span>
  <span id="freeText14677740153189811010" style="display:none">I suppose I've always intended to read Joyce; it's terribly daunting but seems inevitable, too, that I must follow the man all the way through to <i>Finnegans Wake</i>. I have a copy. Untouched. Another remnant of the days when I thought I was on Earth to prove some kind of a point.<br /><br />But I'm still awfully curious, and this year I finally dipped a toe in. <i>Dubliners</i> came first and seemed easiest to start with, and I'd read a story or two of it already. And indeed it is pretty conventional, even self-consciously spare in style.<br /><br />And it is masterful and instantly absorbing. If I were a more serious student of literature I suppose I would know to what extent Joyce is following the narrative mode of the extant literature in his world in 1911, and to what extent he is using narrative devices that are familiar to me only because later writers imitated him. Writing is a sequence of choices, details named amid an infinitude of details omitted, and no matter how terse, flat, and neutral the style, Joyce continually manages to reclaim your attention with virtually every phrase, wasting nothing. Here is part of a character introduction, from <i>A Painful Case</i>:<br /><br /><blockquote>His cheekbones also gave his face a harsh character; but there was no harshness in the eyes which, looking at the world from under their tawny eyebrows, gave the impression of a man ever alert to greet a redeeming instinct in others but often disappointed. He lived at a little distance from his body, regarding his own acts with doubtful side-glances. He had an odd autobiographical habit which led him to compose in his mind from time to time a short sentence about himself containing a subject in the third person and a predicate in the past tense. He never gave alms to beggars and walked firmly, carrying a stout hazel.</blockquote><br /><br />The stories often seem principally to be character sketches, or exercises in placing ultimately regrettable behaviors in their context to explore and explain them. An ambitious youth goes to foolish excesses in order to live for a night in the style of cosmopolitan foreigners. A conniving matron bullies her daughter's beau into a marriage he doesn't want. A mousy, straitlaced man, suddenly feeling trapped in a dull and shameful life, commits a minor, real, mean act, immediately regretted; the next story puts this into perspective with the narrative of a day leading up to outright cruelty.<br /><br />These things pervade the book, in fact. Dublin is dull and shameful; Dubliners long to leave but cannot. And through all the impediments of church and class and poverty that dog them all, drunkenness recurs again and again, prominent in nearly every story. A bit of reading outside the borders of the text tells me that Joyce, terribly particular about every detail of his writing, intended the book as a moral indictment of the people of Dublin and of Ireland as a whole, and that in fact he left Dublin forever within a short time of the book's publication, settling in France where he wrote the rest of his works. So in an important sense this book isn't meant for me, and it's hard to know how much Joyce was leaning on images and phrases that Dubliners of his day would have found familiar, beyond the occasional Irish word or idiom that I can't quite follow. And it is largely a condemnation, in the end, of the city and its people. I can't say whether he meant the book--his parting shot to his native country--to shine a light on Dublin's problems and inspire people to improve them, or if he thought his countrymen hopeless and just had to tell them how much they vexed him.<br /><br />At last in <i>The Dead</i> the narrative looks more or less directly at this underlying discontent. Gabriel Conroy is not so unlike Joyce himself: an urbane, cultured writer with one foot out of Ireland and a clear discomfort even with visiting it for the holidays. He is a subtle and likable character, sympathetically portrayed. But when a young woman calls him out for his alienation from his own country, he is too easily rattled; so deep is his discomfort with his home that he cannot stop himself from exclaiming "I'm sick of my own country, sick of it!" Why, she asks him, and then: "of course, you've no answer." Indeed he hasn't.<br /><br />Does Joyce? Is he putting himself on the spot, here, and admitting that he doesn't really have an answer either? Or if he described his own book as a moral index of his country, should we take its chapters as a proxy answer for Gabriel Conroy: that he, and his author, are sick of Ireland because everyone there is mired in poverty and alcohol and the parochial concerns of their little lives? It's difficult to tell here whether Joyce judges the conversation in favor of Gabriel, who seems evasive and troubled in his conscience, or Miss Ivors, who may be impolitic but who has Gabriel sussed. What is clear, from Joyce's own life, is that Gabriel is the one he must identify with. In his Dublin, every character either longs to escape "dirty old Dublin" or is plainly presented as small-minded in some way. They're all sick of it, and Joyce can't quite spell out why. At least not clearly enough for this reader, a hundred years later.<br /><br />All that said, it's an excellent read, one of those cases where the canons of the ivory-tower literati are so powerfully vindicated that I fret whether I should just accept their judgments every time. <i>Dubliners</i> is so powerful and assured that I have to give it five stars just for the execution of it. But the message--I guess the message might just not be meant for the likes of me.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14677740153189811010" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1070142325" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1070142325" />
    <a title="Srividya" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/27599745-srividya"><img alt="Srividya" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1499431918p2/27599745.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1070142325?book_show_action=true">Jan 05, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Srividya" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Srividya" href="/user/show/27599745-srividya">Srividya</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/23291.Dubliners">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27599745-srividya?shelf=kindle-owned">kindle-owned</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27599745-srividya?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27599745-srividya?shelf=ireland">ireland</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27599745-srividya?shelf=literature">literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27599745-srividya?shelf=2017-reading-challenge">2017-reading-challenge</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27599745-srividya?shelf=short-stories">short-stories</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1070142325" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1357127010804505482">My first ever Joyce and I have to say that I approached this book with a lot of trepidation and yet a curious feeling that I just can’t describe but one can associate with such authors and their books. With Finnegan’s Wake and Ulysses on my ‘I hope to read and understand someday’ shelf, given their notoriety for their abstract and difficult prose, it is no surprise that one would approach Joyce with such feelings. Nevertheless, I picked this one up for two reasons. Firstly, because I am visiting</span>
  <span id="freeText1357127010804505482" style="display:none">My first ever Joyce and I have to say that I approached this book with a lot of trepidation and yet a curious feeling that I just can’t describe but one can associate with such authors and their books. With Finnegan’s Wake and Ulysses on my ‘I hope to read and understand someday’ shelf, given their notoriety for their abstract and difficult prose, it is no surprise that one would approach Joyce with such feelings. Nevertheless, I picked this one up for two reasons. Firstly, because I am visiting Ireland this month and wanted to read Joyce before I could walk the paths that he might have done as a child and secondly because my trip would first lead me to Dublin and how better to understand the city than through Joyce’s wonderful treatise on its locals. There was another third reason, a minor one at that, that this book had short stories and I felt that short stories should be easier to understand and read than a full length book. How wrong was I, I knew only after I started it. <br /><br />As I read the first few lines, despite my fear, I felt an urge to submit myself to this author’s words, to let go of all my inhibitions, my worries about understanding the inner meaning or metaphors used, and to simply fall in love with the words that were seen on my reader. I did that and I was mesmerised. Don’t get me wrong, for all the beauty that I could see in Dubliners, I found a lot of things that irritated me and yet despite these irritations or maybe because of it, Dublin and her people came alive to me. I felt as though I was walking through those roads, visiting those places, drinking tea or stout amidst those very people who shared their stories with me. <br /><br />One of my biggest irritants, which is also my reason for falling in love, was the fact that Joyce doesn’t allow you to be the shy bystander looking in. He pulls you into conversations where you have no reason to be included, and worse he pulls you in when the conversation has already begun and is going on in full force. Further, he never explains the background or even the beginning of the conversation. You feel as if you have literally fallen into a hole where there is a lot of buzzing around you and you have to make sense of this buzz. Added to this, is the fact that Joyce pulls you out before you can get to the end of this conversation. In short, what all this means is that there is no proper beginning, no middle and no end to the story. All this is actually enough to put off a reader as it might often seem and sometimes it truly does that there is no sense in what is being said or what is happening. However, he manages to convey important messages despite this flaw or maybe because of it, you tend to start thinking a little bit more than usual, about the narrator, the narrative, the way it is being narrated and the abrupt end. Once your thoughts go deep, there is an innumerable amount of permutations and combinations for you to choose from, the creative pool is quite deep and it doesn’t matter what you infer from your plunge into this pool, the meaning will be constant, even if your paths differ. Such is the beauty of Joyce’s prose, such is his talent that even when you are ready to kill someone, you end up putting down your arms and embracing that very person and beaming while you are doing it. <br /><br />This does not mean that Joyce’s prose is happy or even cheerful. No these stories are all dark, depressing and pretty dreary. They talk about the three stages of life – child, youth and the middle aged moving towards the old and finally culminating in death. The tone is morose, the tale is sad, the characters are full of angst and dreariness and yet you come out of it satisfied, if not happy; learning more if not really a scholar; emotional, if not really bursting into tears. His stories moved me, especially a few where I couldn’t stop my eyes from growing wet and full. These weren’t tears of sadness but an emotion that I can’t quite place but would rank as a beautiful experience. At the end of the stories, my mind wasn’t my own but were of those characters, so much so that when I finished the book, I felt as if I were leaving behind my closest friends. Whatever I might feel about the individual stories, I will have to accept that each and every one of them brought out emotions in me that were long dormant and this I owe to the beautiful prose. Taut with tension, stingy with truth or completeness, buried in an assault of emotions or in some cases nary an emotion, the stories felt alive. I was living in Dublin, walking through those very streets and looking into the lives of these characters and being one with them. <br /><br />I have experienced Ireland’s green vales and beautiful landscapes in other books and was expecting some of that. Instead, I got the dreary Ireland that is full of pain and remorse, anger and ineptitude, and yet somehow it shone like a loving star, a loving human star – full of faults, full of emotions and full of the Irish nature that I have come to love through books. It takes immense talent to convince someone who isn’t part of it that what is shown is beautiful and Joyce is immensely talented as he does it with ease. You may not like his way of storytelling, you may not like the stories themselves but you can never say that you didn’t feel anything for the country that he wrote about, whether it is love, empathy, sympathy or even disdain, you feel it and feel it strongly as you put down this book. <br /><br />I won’t say that this trip through Joyce’s prose has made me fear him less. In fact, it has made my fear more as every word has a meaning and a reason for why it is there and I am not sure that I am or would ever be capable of understanding it all. However, this trip has definitely made me understand why Joyce is called a difficult author to read and comprehend. It has helped me see a little into his prowess and his storytelling. It has made me love him for all that and fear him more for the same. And in the end, it has left me eager to walk through those lanes, albeit several years later, it could be different, and yet I am truly eager to get there and pay my respects to the land that was described in this book.<br /><br />As I write this on the penultimate day before I leave for Ireland, I have a sense of peace and some knowledge, the country might be different but people everywhere are the same. While I go pack for my trip, why don’t you take this trip through Joyce’s eyes. I can’t say that all of you will love it but even if one person does, it will be a wonderful thing to both Joyce and me. And if you can’t read the whole book, at least read Araby, The Dead, Counterparts, A Little Cloud and A Painful Case. I promise you that it will be worth your time. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="1357127010804505482" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3411674146" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4100255307379676332">Very tempted now to go through all of Joyce again; how different this was in my memory. I mean, it was great, it was Joyce after all: but this is surely the best single volume of stories in literature. (And, according to a John Banville foreword to a different version I have on the shelves, quite possibly his own personal favourite of his own works.)<br/><br/>'The air of the room chilled his shoulders. He stretched himself cautiously along under the sheets and lay down beside his wife. One by one, they we</span>
  <span id="freeText4100255307379676332" style="display:none">Very tempted now to go through all of Joyce again; how different this was in my memory. I mean, it was great, it was Joyce after all: but this is surely the best single volume of stories in literature. (And, according to a John Banville foreword to a different version I have on the shelves, quite possibly his own personal favourite of his own works.)<br /><br />'The air of the room chilled his shoulders. He stretched himself cautiously along under the sheets and lay down beside his wife. One by one, they were all becoming shades. Better pass boldly into that other world, in the full glory of some passion, than fade and wither dismally with age. He thought of how she who lay beside him had locked in her heart for so many years that image of her lover's eyes when he had told her that he did not wish to live. <br /><br />Generous tears filled Gabriel's eyes. He had never felt like that himself towards any woman, but he knew that such a feeling must be love. The tears gathered more thickly in his eyes and in the partial darkness he imagined he saw the form of a young man standing under a dripping tree. Other forms were near. His soul had approached that region where dwell the vast hosts of the dead. He was conscious of, but could not apprehend, their wayward and flickering existence. His own identity was fading out into a grey impalpable world: the solid world itself, which these dead had one time reared and lived in, was dissolving and dwindling. <br /><br />A few light taps upon the pane made him turn to the window. It had begun to snow again. He watched sleepily the flakes, silver and dark, falling obliquely against the lamplight. The time had come for him to set out on his journey westward. Yes, the newspapers were right: snow was general all over Ireland. It was falling on every part of the dark central plain, on the treeless hills, falling softly upon the Bog of Allen and, farther westward, softly falling into the dark mutinous Shannon waves. It was falling, too, upon every part of the lonely churchyard on the hill where Michael Furey lay buried. It lay thickly drifted on the crooked crosses and headstones, on the spears of the little gate, on the barren thorns. His soul swooned slowly as he heard the snow falling faintly through the universe and faintly falling, like the descent of their last end, upon all the living and the dead.'</span>
  <a data-text-id="4100255307379676332" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground">Readers also enjoyed</h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent"><div class='bookCarousel'>
<div class='carouselRow' style='width: 3600px'>
<ul>
<li class='cover' id='bookCover_59716'>
<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/59716.To_the_Lighthouse"><img alt="To the Lighthouse" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/books/1346239665l/59716._SX98_.jpg" />
</a>

  
<script type="text/javascript" charset="utf-8">
//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('8aoq3PGuAQ+yeuYJO+WIjTpIbQy1ZpkBv/D2Gzhf3kgXerefE6fklVVVZSEu02rlRFbUDFan8/Tnw/Pc1lK/UA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {

Flaubert\'s erotically charged and psychologically acute portrayal of Emma Bovary caused a moral outcry on its publication in 1857.       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/5144.James_Joyce">About James Joyce</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/5144.James_Joyce"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1517863935p3/5144.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/5144.James_Joyce">James Joyce
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
6,467
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer17231242576298941618">James Joyce, Irish novelist, noted for his experimental use of language in such works as <i>Ulysses</i> (1922) and <i>Finnegans Wake</i> (1939). Joyce's technical innovations in the art of the novel include an extensive use of interior monologue; he used a complex network of symbolic parallels drawn from the mythology, history, and literature, and created a unique language of invented words, puns, and allusions</span>
  <span id="freeText17231242576298941618" style="display:none">James Joyce, Irish novelist, noted for his experimental use of language in such works as <i>Ulysses</i> (1922) and <i>Finnegans Wake</i> (1939). Joyce's technical innovations in the art of the novel include an extensive use of interior monologue; he used a complex network of symbolic parallels drawn from the mythology, history, and literature, and created a unique language of invented words, puns, and allusions. <br /><br />James Joyce was born in Dublin, on February 2, 1882, the eldest of ten surviving siblings, two other died of typhoid. His father was John Stanislaus Joyce, an impoverished gentleman, who had failed in a distillery business and tried all kinds of other professions, including politics and tax collecting. Joyce's mother, Mary Jane Murray, was an accomplished pianist, whose life was dominated by the Roman Catholic Church. In spite of their poverty, the family struggled to maintain a solid middle-class facade.<br /><br />From the age of six Joyce, was educated by Jesuits at Clongowes Wood College, at Clane, and then at Belvedere College in Dublin (1893-97). In 1898 he entered the University College, Dublin. Joyce's first publication was an essay on Ibsen's play <i>When We Dead Awaken</i>. It appeared in the <i>Fortnightly Review</i> in 1900. At this time he also began writing lyric poems.<br /><br />After graduation in 1902 the twenty-year-old Joyce went to Paris, where he worked as a journalist, teacher and in other occupations under difficult financial conditions. He spent a year in France, returning when a telegram arrived saying his mother was dying. Not long after her death, Joyce was traveling again. He left Dublin in 1904 with Nora Barnacle, a chambermaid who he married in 1931. <br /><br />Joyce published <i>Dubliners</i> in 1914, <i>A Portrait of the Artist as a Young Man</i> in 1916, a play <i>Exiles</i> in 1918 and <i>Ulysses</i> in 1922. In 1907 Joyce had published a collection of poems, <i>Chamber Music</i>.<br /><br />At the outset of the First World War, Joyce moved with his family to Zürich. In Zürich Joyce started to develop the early chapters of Ulysses, which was first published in France because of censorship troubles in the Great Britain and the United States, where the book became legally available only in 1933. In March 1923 Joyce started in Paris his second major work, <i>Finnegans Wake</i>, suffering at the same time chronic eye troubles caused by glaucoma. The first segment of the novel appeared in Ford Madox Ford's transatlantic review in April 1924, as part of what Joyce called Work in Progress. The final version was published in 1939.<br /><br />Some critics considered the work a masterpiece, though many readers found it incomprehensible. After the fall of France in World War II, Joyce returned to Zürich, where he died on January 13, 1941, still disappointed with the reception of <i>Finnegans Wake</i>.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17231242576298941618" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>

      <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/work/quotes/260248-dubliners">Quotes from Dubliners</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;A few light taps upon the pane made him turn to the window. It had begun to snow again. He watched sleepily the flakes, silver and dark, falling obliquely against the lamplight. The time had come for him to set out on his journey westward. Yes, the newspapers were right: snow was general all over Ireland. It was falling on every part of the dark central plain, on the treeless hills, falling softly upon the Bog of Allen and, farther westward, softly falling into the dark mutinous Shannon waves. It was falling, too, upon every part of the lonely churchyard on the hill where Michael Furey lay buried. It lay thickly drifted on the crooked crosses and headstones, on the spears of the little gate, on the barren thorns. His soul swooned slowly as he heard the snow falling faintly through the universe and faintly falling, like the descent of their last end, upon all the living and the dead.&rdquo;</span>
